    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11207577843778169991">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)<br/><br/>The novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, a</span>
  <span id="freeText11207577843778169991" style="display:none">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)<br /><br />The novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, and that he shares his evil destiny: he is born to be a victim of the State. Because of his stories about Džem’s ambitions to overthrow his brother, Ćamil is arrested under suspicion of plotting against the Sultan. He is taken to a prison in Istanbul, where he tells his story, to Petar, a monk.<br /><br />Out of these exotic materials, Andrić has constructed a book of great clarity, brevity and interest. No doubt it will be read by some as a political parable about the tyranny of the State, but also as a quite simply story about ill-fortune and human misunderstanding, fear and ignorance. Džem and Ćamil are doomed – and the certainty of their persecution is sometimes relieved, sometimes intensified by the stupidity and fright of the people who cross their ill-starred lives.<br /><br />Construction takes up most of the book’s space: the central story of Džem as related by Ćamil lasts only a chapter or two. For the rest of the time the reader strips layer off layer, as one narrator passes him on the next. There is an interesting passage that helps to explain this method, at the moment when Ćamil starts narrating Džem’s story in the first person. “I” is a word, we are told, which fixes the position of the speaker in such a way that the exercise of will is no longer possible, and the speaker strength is exceeded – strength, presumably, to break out of the identification that all his past actions and thoughts force upon him when he uses the word. “I” is both a confession and an imprisonment. The fact that the novel passes the reader on from one narrator to the next rather suggests that the author is taking constant evasive action, lest he betray himself or his reader into the kind of “personal confession” which seals the fate of Ćamil. What exactly this game of form flirting with meaning signifies, must be left to the individual reader.<br /><br />The movement is centripetal, towards Džem’s story, and then disperses. Details within the story are made to mimic this form. Thus when Peter receives the message telling him of his impending release:<br /><br />“Two younger prisoners...were chasing around using him as the centerpoint of ever narrowing circles. Annoyed, he tried to break away from these exuberant youths when one of them brushed against him and he felt a folded scrap of paper thrust into his hand. The youths continued their chase but now in widening circles...”<br /><br />The reader is led on just such a chase in the course of the novel. The effect of this is to make the plot seem more like a poetic image than an ordinary plot: capable, therefore, of as many meanings as are the images of an allusive poem. Yet the language is simple and direct, not at all “poetic”. The characters are remarkable alive, even in conversation. Karađoz, the governor of the goal, is a spidery authoritarian, who loves to torment the charges he loves. The prisoners “complained about the way one complains about one’s life and curses one’s destiny...it would have been hard for them to imagine life without him”.<br /><br />“The Devil’s Yard” is justified, as all symbolic and figurative novels must be, by the extent to which it touches the emotions. It is extremely moving. Fear, horror, despair, amusement at times – all these indicate that the threat of the meaning has been recognized.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11207577843778169991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});

      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2645802042" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2645802042" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2645802042?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781856100229" href="/book/show/476383.The_Damned_Yard">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2645802042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3814018306873447599">What is left after a man dies?<br/><blockquote>People engaged in computing the estate of a dead man who, only a couple of days ago, was as alive as they are now, have a peculiar look. They represent victorious life, which goes its own way and has its own necessities. But, victors of a pretty poor sort, their only merit consists in their having outlived the dead. They remind one of looters, but looters who are sure to go scot free and who know that the rightful owner can never return and catch them. Not that they</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText3814018306873447599" style="display:none">What is left after a man dies?<br /><blockquote>People engaged in computing the estate of a dead man who, only a couple of days ago, was as alive as they are now, have a peculiar look. They represent victorious life, which goes its own way and has its own necessities. But, victors of a pretty poor sort, their only merit consists in their having outlived the dead. They remind one of looters, but looters who are sure to go scot free and who know that the rightful owner can never return and catch them. Not that they are quite that, of course, but somehow they give you that feeling.</blockquote><br />An old monk died but after his death he has left not just his poor earthly belongings but also his stories. His memories of being imprisoned in the place of detention in Istanbul, notoriously known as the <i>Devil’s Yard</i>. “To this place there came – and through it there passed, day by day – all who were arrested or taken into custody in this sprawling and populous city, for offenses committed or upon suspicion of offense…”<br />In some ways <i>The Damned Yard</i> by <b>Ivo Andrić</b> reminds of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49451.The_House_of_the_Dead" title="The House of the Dead by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Dead</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>.<br />Stories of misery and despair... Stories of madness... Stories within stories...<br /><blockquote>Here were offenders both great and small, from the lad who had lifted a bunch of grapes or a fig from a counter, to swindlers and burglars; the innocent were here, and the falsely accused, the weak-witted and the betrayed, or people brought here by mistake from Istanbul and from all over the Empire… <br />There were men with many murders to their account and others with records of prison escapes who were therefore already in irons here, even before their trial and conviction. They clanked their chains provocatively, with furious curses on all iron and on the man who first thought of fetters.</blockquote><br />And the central is the story of a lonesome young Turk who has been falsely accused of conspiracy against the Sultan.<br />In all the nations, prisons are similar to one another. In all the times, methods of the secret police remain the same.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3814018306873447599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/940220088" />
    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/940220088?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781856100229" href="/book/show/476383.The_Damned_Yard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327886-jonfaith?shelf=balkan">balkan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer940220088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3922835369694152836"><b>But let me come straight to the point. Bosnia is a wonderful country, fascinating, with nothing ordinary in the habitat or people. And just as there are mineral riches under the earth in Bosnia, so undoubtedly are Bosnians rich in hidden moral values, which are more rarely found in their compatriots in other Yugoslav lands. But, you see, there's one thing that the people of Bosnia, at least people of your kind, must realize and never lose sight of--Bosnia is a country of hatred and fear.</b><br/><br/>The deva</span>
  <span id="freeText3922835369694152836" style="display:none"><b>But let me come straight to the point. Bosnia is a wonderful country, fascinating, with nothing ordinary in the habitat or people. And just as there are mineral riches under the earth in Bosnia, so undoubtedly are Bosnians rich in hidden moral values, which are more rarely found in their compatriots in other Yugoslav lands. But, you see, there's one thing that the people of Bosnia, at least people of your kind, must realize and never lose sight of--Bosnia is a country of hatred and fear.</b><br /><br />The devastating floods which smashed through Bosnia and Serbia over the weekend shadowed my reading of these stories. During the brightest of summer days these tales would depress most anyone. Factoring in the present suffering overseas made them often unbearable. I have only slept a single night in Bosnia but I did visit Andric's apartment in Belgrade last year. I doubt if any of that informs much, but it remains interesting. There are floods in this collection. There is also treachery. What reigns though is the supremacy and indifference of mortality. The stories, except for the titular one, are set in Bosnia. The Damned Yard occurs in a Turkish prison in Stamboul.<br /><br />What remains strange about this collection is a single omission. There are priests and viziers, merchants and drunks. There are artists and investigators, but there is a dearth of revolutionaries. I can't help but ponder whether that mirrors or distorts any thoughts or preconditions by the masterful diplomat, Nobel Laureate and writer. </span>

<div id="review_1173586589" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1173586589" />
    <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur"><img alt="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602184090p2/16951301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1173586589?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur" href="/user/show/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur">Bryan--Pumpkin Connoisseur</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/20094046-the-damned-yard-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=books-i-own-and-have-read">books-i-own-and-have-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16951301-bryan--pumpkin-connoisseur?shelf=books-i-read-in-2015">books-i-read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1173586589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8867327184701942529">This is the second work I've read of Andric, the first being one of his more prominent novels, <i>The Bridge on the Drina</i>. Both of these works are very similar, as the novel was a collection of stories centered on the community on the Drina river, and <i>The Damned Yard</i> a collection of stories which centered on Bosnia during the rule of the Ottoman Turks. One could say that the novel had more of a central theme to keep it together, though in essence, there seemed little that would have prevented Andri</span>
  <span id="freeText8867327184701942529" style="display:none">This is the second work I've read of Andric, the first being one of his more prominent novels, <i>The Bridge on the Drina</i>. Both of these works are very similar, as the novel was a collection of stories centered on the community on the Drina river, and <i>The Damned Yard</i> a collection of stories which centered on Bosnia during the rule of the Ottoman Turks. One could say that the novel had more of a central theme to keep it together, though in essence, there seemed little that would have prevented Andric from fitting in the stories from <i>The Damned Yard</i> into <i>The Drina</i>.<br /><br />I don't know if I have the ability to communicate exactly what it was that I found so entrancing about Andric's stories--they are deceptively simple; just folk stories really. And yet somehow the author was able to draw me into this world of which I have no first-hand knowledge (and little second-hand), and make it fascinating. One thought that struck a glancing blow as I was reading was how real these people seemed--real without the artificial, satirical layers that the contemporary world imposes on us. I feel sometimes that what we think we know about human nature has progressed to where we are no longer able to appreciate its intricacies, and the characters that populate Andric's stories are unburdened by psychology and other inhibiting information. Therefore they can, at times, appear child-like, yet at the same time freer.<br /><br />Well, as I say--it's not a simple thing to describe why these stories have their appeal. Other readers may find them unsophisticated--and they are; wonderfully so. And also deeply affecting. Andric writes about another way to live life, a way that I feel most of the people I know would rebuff. But in Andric's hands, it can seem liberating.<br /><br />I look forward to reading more by Andric--it has been a singular pleasure for me to read what I have so far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8867327184701942529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781856100229" href="/book/show/476383.The_Damned_Yard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51836895-ivana?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1907006183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16426452121256710407">The Damned Yard, or Prokleta Avlija originally is a short book by Ivo Andrić, the literature nobelist. I definitely wasn't very excited when i saw this book on my school reading list. I never quite liked the concept of Andrić and found his work dull.<br/><br/><br/>Needless to say I was surprised when I really liked this short book. It was very gripping and filled with the most interesting stories of ordinary and nothing but ordinary people. I almost screamed when I stumbled upon one of my all time favorite top</span>
  <span id="freeText16426452121256710407" style="display:none">The Damned Yard, or Prokleta Avlija originally is a short book by Ivo Andrić, the literature nobelist. I definitely wasn't very excited when i saw this book on my school reading list. I never quite liked the concept of Andrić and found his work dull.<br /><br /><br />Needless to say I was surprised when I really liked this short book. It was very gripping and filled with the most interesting stories of ordinary and nothing but ordinary people. I almost screamed when I stumbled upon one of my all time favorite topics - The Borgias. The writing was exquisite and luxurious. I couldn't stop reading and ended up finishing the book in less than 30 minutes.<br /><br />The book tells the story of inhabitants of a prison called Prokleta Avlija - The Damned Yard. The characters are intriguing and have very vivid back-stories which obviously build their personalities.<br /><br />In conclusion, I would recommend this book, so if it is unavoidable to read it do not be as presumptuous and prejudiced as me and simply dive into it.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://bookprejudice.wordpress.com/2017/02/09/ivo-andric-the-damned-yard-review/">https://bookprejudice.wordpress.com/2...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16426452121256710407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8522704844225339729">Absolutely loved it! Ivo Andrić is definitely writer I'll read more from. <br/><br/>The outer frame of Andrić's narrative, which is a system of concentric circles - the story of a story, is a scene of fra Peter's funeral. Snow covers the grave and this is what remains: <br/> <br/><i>"the general whiteness which stretched as far as the eye could see merged imperceptibly<br/> with the grey desert of the sky still full of snow."</i><br/><br/>Entering world of Devils Yard, we encounter people of all creeds, vocations and age. They are mad o</span>
  <span id="freeText8522704844225339729" style="display:none">Absolutely loved it! Ivo Andrić is definitely writer I'll read more from. <br /><br />The outer frame of Andrić's narrative, which is a system of concentric circles - the story of a story, is a scene of fra Peter's funeral. Snow covers the grave and this is what remains: <br /> <br /><i>"the general whiteness which stretched as far as the eye could see merged imperceptibly<br /> with the grey desert of the sky still full of snow."</i><br /><br />Entering world of Devils Yard, we encounter people of all creeds, vocations and age. They are mad or sane, innocent or guilty.<br /><br />Karagoz(the governor of the Turkish prison), a dark shadow of the human mind, is driven by the animalism and abnormality into a sadistic behavior toward the world he once belonged to. A mentally deranged person and a criminal chases and persecutes the prisoners who are destined to suffer. <br />He fights his own, socially undesirable, instincts attacking others for what he feels.<br /><br />Andrić uses prison an an archetypal motive alluding to human life : <br /> <br /> <i>"So the Yard ceaselessly sifts the variegated crowd of its population and, always full, it is constantly being filled and emptied anew."</i><br /><br /><br /><br />Definitely worth reading. Would recommend for everyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8522704844225339729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2051503739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2051503739" />
    <a title="John Farebrother" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother"><img alt="John Farebrother" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499497213p2/68609816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2051503739?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Farebrother" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Farebrother" href="/user/show/68609816-john-farebrother">John Farebrother</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2051503739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17633166838022868754">The powerful visual quality of this book always reminds me of the Balkan refugee crisis. It is a drama set in an Ottoman remand centre in Istanbul, where a Bosnian Catholic priest is detained for being in the wrong place at the wrong time. But like everything in the Balkans, there is far more to it than that. In many respects it tells the tale of the history and power relations within the Balkans, which are as relevant today as is the 1990s refugee crisis. Above all, it is evocative of wartime B</span>
  <span id="freeText17633166838022868754" style="display:none">The powerful visual quality of this book always reminds me of the Balkan refugee crisis. It is a drama set in an Ottoman remand centre in Istanbul, where a Bosnian Catholic priest is detained for being in the wrong place at the wrong time. But like everything in the Balkans, there is far more to it than that. In many respects it tells the tale of the history and power relations within the Balkans, which are as relevant today as is the 1990s refugee crisis. Above all, it is evocative of wartime Bosnia, with its multifaceted society and cultural heritage.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17633166838022868754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1996382396" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1996382396" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1996382396?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/35126454-la-cour-maudite">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who have read the Bosnian Trilogy</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=southern-slav-greek-lit">southern-slav-greek-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1996382396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15044353140057555189">Prokleta Avlija (the Damned Yard) will be a great source of pleasure to anyone who has read and enjoyed Ivo Andric's Bosnian Trilogy (comprised of the Bridge on the Drina, the Woman from Sarajevo and the Bosnian Chronicles). However, if one has not read at least the first volume (the Bridge on the Drina), Prokleta Avlija will be a confusing and highly disappointing work.<br/><br/>Prokleta Avlija is a collection of five tales in which Brother Peter an elderly Franciscan monk imparts his wisdom gained from </span>
  <span id="freeText15044353140057555189" style="display:none">Prokleta Avlija (the Damned Yard) will be a great source of pleasure to anyone who has read and enjoyed Ivo Andric's Bosnian Trilogy (comprised of the Bridge on the Drina, the Woman from Sarajevo and the Bosnian Chronicles). However, if one has not read at least the first volume (the Bridge on the Drina), Prokleta Avlija will be a confusing and highly disappointing work.<br /><br />Prokleta Avlija is a collection of five tales in which Brother Peter an elderly Franciscan monk imparts his wisdom gained from an eventful life to a young generation of monks. The key to the group of stories can be found in the fourth tale (the Cup). Peter a young monk is about to flee the monastery but his stopped by his mentor Father Nicolas who explains to him that he was meant for the monastic world and can never be happy in the normal world. Fortunately, Father Peter decides to heed this wise council.<br /><br />The other four stories recount trips made by monks away from the monastery. In only one of the four cases do the Brothers actually succeed on the objective they set out with. In all four instances they encounter horrors and retreat back to monastery where peace awaits them.<br /><br />The Damned Yard is by far the longest and most ambitious of the four tales. A victim of mistaken identity, Brother Peter is arrested and thrown into the Ottoman Prison in Istanbul where he spends two months before he is liberated. The simple philosophy of the prison warden is that no one either in the prison or out of it is innocent. Brother Peter who somewhat deceitfully conceals his identity as a Catholic clergyman fearing that the Islamic jailers will be more cruel to him, if they know what he his. Father Peter is befriended by a paranoid Jew who informs him that everyone in the Prison is an informer. Among the other prisoners, Father Peter is most drawn to a delusional young Turkish nobleman who believes himself to be the Sultan and accordingly has been jailed by Ottoman officials who are afraid that he might be part of a revolutionary conspiracy. Brother Peter who ultimately spends very little time in the prison never learns what happens to his mentally unstable friend.<br /><br />The Damned Yard is difficult to interpret. Andric's native city of Travnik only ceased to be part of the Ottoman empire in 1878 less than 20 years prior to Andric's birth so the story could be commentary on the Ottoman carceral system especially given that a major focus of Andric's work was the impact of Ottoman culture on his native country. However, Andric was arrested and briefly jailed for suspected involvement in the assassination of the Archiduke Franz Ferdinand in 1914, so the work could also be about the Austro-Hungarian prison system. Finally, the themes of the overall story collection suggest the Andric might also be talking about the essential evil of the world outside the monastery.<br /><br />The Damned Yard is very delightful and interesting work but it is not an entry portal into the work of Andric. The reader who does not read the novels in the Bosnian trilogy first will have great difficulty entering into the imaginary universe of this very eccentric set of short stories. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15044353140057555189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2180378540687894994">This book was ok, nothing more. A couple of men, a clergyman, a Jew and a Turk of Greek heritage meet in prison and tell get to know their lives' stories. They're sad, and end up tragically: the Turk presumably passes away and the Jew gets a mental illness. However, that's it. The style is very conservative and fits well with the little boring plot. Similarly to "the Bridge over the Drina", where the bridge is the main character, in this book it is the prison. There's no real metaphoric masterpi</span>
  <span id="freeText2180378540687894994" style="display:none">This book was ok, nothing more. A couple of men, a clergyman, a Jew and a Turk of Greek heritage meet in prison and tell get to know their lives' stories. They're sad, and end up tragically: the Turk presumably passes away and the Jew gets a mental illness. However, that's it. The style is very conservative and fits well with the little boring plot. Similarly to "the Bridge over the Drina", where the bridge is the main character, in this book it is the prison. There's no real metaphoric masterpiece to find in here, which goes well with the depressing atmosphere in prisons in general. This book is somewhat logical, but disappointing as well to me, given that from reading one impressing short story by the author I know that Andric could do better than that. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1055530697911901283">The book "Devil's Yard" is about a Serbian man's experience in a Turkish prison. The book is pretty slow and philosophical. While reading this I was reminded how formulaic prison books and movies are. In this story a man friends and newly incarcerated intellectual who is innocent (sound familiar) and the two grow a strong bond and try to survive the harsh conditions set by the corrupt warden.<br/><br/>I feel like the writing of Andric is lost in translation and it is not his own translation.   <span id="freeText1055530697911901283" style="display:none">The book "Devil's Yard" is about a Serbian man's experience in a Turkish prison. The book is pretty slow and philosophical. While reading this I was reminded how formulaic prison books and movies are. In this story a man friends and newly incarcerated intellectual who is innocent (sound familiar) and the two grow a strong bond and try to survive the harsh conditions set by the corrupt warden.<br /><br />I feel like the writing of Andric is lost in translation and it is not his own translation. Some of the sentences were extremely awkward and not grammatically correct. Many of his books were translated to english right after he won the Nobel Prize. So the translation might have been rushed to capitalize on book sales.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer647499746" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13085166678677250804">What a fantastic book of translated short stories, I couldn't put it down during my flight home from Belgrade; I just re-read it during vacation. The common theme through the selection and topic of the stories is that of story-telling itself. The main story is 'The Damned Yard,' itself told as a story about story-telling. I've been thinking of how it could be adapted for the stage, might be my next project...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1523350731" />

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1523350731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5256525883697169327">This collection by the Nobel Prize-winner from Bosnia is formidable. A couple of the stories were, at least for me, close to impenetrable, but the majority were transporting. The title story -- the longest in the collection -- is a luminous, even visceral, celebration of the necessity of story-telling.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tD5t2+sPTltO5aRgTDcrGotsipgm6p38UcOCycwZSHHIxp6700XQOXRoUfWmf1krU7dxa6UkFWWDjtuoipFgTA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CKrwtQ+ZsJKi7Jo/9YY6puDRmQzFwRNdW+skkiOXs/l0UgPVN9Mu8Jhhb6ofzkiXOApi/0YPm8SJpn3zZR+bxA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_">About Ivo Andrić</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1212894919p3/2147.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_">Ivo Andrić
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
953
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5425634779561004496">Ivan "Ivo" Andrić (Cyrillic: Иво Андрић) was a Yugoslav and Bosnian novelist, short story writer and Nobel prizewinner. His writings deal mainly with life in his native Bosnia under the Ottoman Empire. His house in Travnik is now a Museum. His Belgrade flat on Andrićev Venac hosts the Museum of Ivo Andrić and the Ivo Andrić Foundation.<br/><br/>After the Second World War, he spent most of his time in his Be</span>
  <span id="freeText5425634779561004496" style="display:none">Ivan "Ivo" Andrić (Cyrillic: Иво Андрић) was a Yugoslav and Bosnian novelist, short story writer and Nobel prizewinner. His writings deal mainly with life in his native Bosnia under the Ottoman Empire. His house in Travnik is now a Museum. His Belgrade flat on Andrićev Venac hosts the Museum of Ivo Andrić and the Ivo Andrić Foundation.<br /><br />After the Second World War, he spent most of his time in his Belgrade home, held ceremonial posts in the Communist government of Yugoslavia and was a Bosnia and Herzegovina parliamentarian. He was also a member of the Serbian Academy of Sciences and Arts.<br /><br /> In 1961, he was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature "for the epic force with which he has traced themes and depicted human destinies drawn from the history of his country". He donated the prize money to libraries in Bosnia and Herzegovina.<br /><br />His works include <i>The Bridge on the Drina</i>, <i>Bosnian Chronicle</i> (aka <i>Chronicles of Travnik</i>), and <i>The Woman from Sarajevo</i>. These were written during WW2 while he was living quietly in Belgrade and published in 1945. They are often referred to as the "Bosnian Trilogy" as they were published simultaneously and had been written in the same period. However, they're connected only thematically.<br /><br />Other works include <i>Ex Ponto</i> (1918), <i>Unrest</i> (Nemiri, '20), <i>The Journey of Alija Đerzelez</i> (Put Alije Đerzeleza, 1920), <i>The Vizier's Elephant</i> (Priča o vezirovom slonu, 1948; tr. 1962), <i>The Damned Yard</i> (Prokleta avlija, 1954), and <i>Omer-Pasha Latas </i>(Omerpaša Latas, released posthumously in 1977)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5425634779561004496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

I našoj radosti zbog ponovnog viđenja tada treba malo vremena da se digne s dna, gde je potisnuta, i pojavi na površini.&rdquo;</span>
